import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Pre-norm 1D dilated convolution block: LayerNorm, GELU activation, dilated Conv1d, and residual addition.
    Common in genomic sequence models (Enformer, Basenji) for capturing long-range dependencies in DNA/RNA sequences.
    """
    def __init__(self, channels, seq_len, kernel_size, dilation):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len])
        self.activation = nn.GELU()
        self.conv = nn.Conv1d(channels, channels, kernel_size, padding=dilation, dilation=dilation)

    def forward(self, x):
        residual = x
        x = self.norm(x)
        x = self.activation(x)
        x = self.conv(x)
        x = x + residual
        return x

batch_size = 32
channels = 256
seq_len = 4096
kernel_size = 3
dilation = 4

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, channels, seq_len)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [channels, seq_len, kernel_size, dilation]

reasoning
We need to write a CUDA kernel for the forward pass of a PyTorch model: pre-norm 1D dilated convolution block: LayerNorm over channels and seq_len? Wait, LayerNorm is defined as `nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len])` — this normalizes over the last two dimensions (channels and seq_len) for each batch element. The input shape is (batch_size, channels, seq_len). So LayerNorm computes mean and variance over (channels, seq_len). Then GELU applied elementwise. Then Conv1d with same channels -> channels, kernel_size=3, dilation=4, padding=dilation (which is 4). Wait, Conv1d padding: `padding=dilation` means padding = 4. Since kernel_size=3, dilation=4, the effective kernel size is (kernel_size-1)*dilation + 1 = (3-1)*4+1 = 9. With padding=4 on each side? Actually in Conv1d, padding parameter is added to both sides; the effective input length becomes seq_len + 2*padding = seq_len + 8. Output length = (seq_len + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel_size-1) - 1)/stride + 1 = (seq_len + 8 - 8 - 1)/1 + 1 = seq_len. So output length matches seq_len. Good. After conv, elementwise addition with residual (input x). Output shape same as input: (batch_size, channels, seq_len). Goal: write a fused CUDA kernel that performs LayerNorm + GELU + Dilated Conv1d + ResidualAdd all in one kernel, to reduce memory bandwidth and improve speed. Need to consider the GPU specs. Let's call get_gpu_specs first.

Computation per thread: ```cpp float acc[8]; for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) acc[i] = bias[oc]; for (int ic = 0; ic < 256; ic++) { float w0 = weight[oc][ic][0]; float w1 = weight[oc][ic][1]; float w2 = weight[oc][ic][2]; for (int i = 0; i < 8; i++) { int p = local_pos[i]; float in0 = smem[ic][p_offset - 4]; // need mapping float in1 = smem[ic][p_offset]; float in2 = smem[ic][p_offset + 4]; acc[i] += w0 * in0 + w1 * in1 + w2 * in2; } } ``` Wait, the shared memory indexing. If shared memory is shaped as [num_ic=256][num_spatial=72] (row-major), then smem[ic][pos] is accessed. For 256 threads each doing 8 output positions, and iterating over 256 input channels, we have 256 iterations. In each iteration, all threads in the block access different smem[ic] for their output positions. But for a fixed ic, all threads access the same ic but potentially different spatial positions. Bank conflicts: shared memory is banked. If threads access the same row but different columns, and the columns differ by a multiple of 32, there are bank conflicts. In our case, if thread (oc, g) accesses spatial positions p = g*8 + i for its 8 positions, and since spatial positions might differ across threads, we need to check. Actually, for a given ic and iteration, threads are spread across different output channels (oc varies), and each thread accesses multiple positions. The key is that multiple threads accessing the same ic and different positions - positions that might cause bank conflicts. Shared memory is arranged as smem[ic][spatial]. For float, spatial index maps to banks. Banks are 32-wide for 32-bit data. So spatial positions 0, 32, 64, ... are in the same bank. If 32 threads access spatial positions 0, 1, 2, ..., 31 for the same ic, no bank conflicts. If they access 0, 8, 16, 24, ... might have conflicts depending on alignment. In our case, during the conv computation, thread (oc, g) accesses positions in the shared memory corresponding to its output tile. For a fixed ic, threads with different g access different output position groups. For g=0, positions 0..7; g=1, positions 8..15, etc. For a single iteration over ic: - Warp 0: oc=0..31, g=0. Threads access smem[ic][p0] where p0 varies by thread but all in range [0, 7] for tap0, [4, 11] for tap1, [8, 15] for tap2. - Wait, each thread accumulates 8 positions, and for each position it needs 3 taps. So for each ic, each thread does 8*3 = 24 shared memory reads. Actually, if we structure the inner loop over positions, the reads for a fixed ic are sequential. To avoid bank conflicts, we could have the 32 threads in a warp work on the SAME output position or ADJACENT output channels with the SAME spatial offset. Alternative thread layout: Have the block compute 32 output positions and 8 output channels? Or 64 output positions and 4 output channels? Actually, for the conv, there's no interaction between output channels. Each output channel uses the same input channels but different weights. If threads in a warp work on the same spatial position but different output channels, then when reading from shared memory (same ic, same spatial pos), all threads read the same address! That's a broadcast, which has no bank conflict. Without shared memory, we can have much higher occupancy. What if we don't use shared memory for input either, and rely on L1/L2 cache? If we don't use shared memory: - Each block of 256 threads computes, say, 32 channels x 8 positions = 256 outputs. - Each thread computes 1 output. - Thread reads 256*3 input values for its specific 3 positions. - If many threads in the block access the same input values, the L1 cache will serve them. - A block handling 32 channels x 8 positions: there are 8 distinct spatial positions (output), requiring 8+8=16 input positions. - For each of these 16 positions, 32 output channels need the same 256 input channels at those positions. - Wait, each output channel needs all 256 input channels at 3 positions. So for 32 output channels and 8 positions: - Total distinct input values needed: 256 input channels * 16 spatial positions = 4096 values. - But each thread reads 768 values independently. - Without shared memory, each thread's reads go through L1. L1 cache is 128 KB per SM on H100. 4096 floats = 16 KB. This fits in L1! - So all threads in the block will load their input values, and the L1 cache will capture the reuse across threads. This is promising! With L1 cache serving the input reuse, we don't need shared memory, and we can have much higher occupancy. Block configuration without shared memory: - Block = (32, 8) = 256 threads - threadIdx.x = 0..31: output channel in a group of 32 - threadIdx.y = 0..7: output position in a group of 8 - Block handles 32 output channels and 8 output positions = 256 outputs. - Total blocks = 32 * 8 * 512 = 131,072 blocks. Occupancy is high. L1 cache will help with input reuse within each block. For weights, each thread reads 768 floats per output. Let's think about the access pattern without shared memory: 1. For a given (oc, p), thread reads x[b, ic, p-4], x[b, ic, p], x[b, ic, p+4] for ic=0..255. - For a fixed p and varying ic, these are strided accesses (stride = L = 4096). - For a fixed ic and varying p (within a warp), if threads have different p, accesses are... well, our warp has 32 threads all with the same p but different oc. - Wait, with block=(32,8): - threadIdx.x = oc in 0..31 - threadIdx.y = position group in 0..7 - A warp is 32 consecutive threads. If threadIdx.x varies fastest, then warp 0 consists of threads with threadIdx.y=0 and threadIdx.x=0..31. - So warp 0: 32 threads, all with p = block_p_start + 0, but oc = 0..31. - All threads in warp 0 read the SAME input positions (p-4, p, p+4) but different weights. - This means for a fixed ic, all 32 threads in the warp read x[b, ic, p-4] - the SAME address! This is a broadcast, which is very cache-friendly. - Then x[b, ic, p], same address for all threads. - Then x[b, ic, p+4], same address for all threads. - This is EXCELLENT for L1 cache! One L1 load serves all 32 threads. For weights: - Thread oc reads weight[oc, ic, k] for k=0..2. - Warp reads weight[0, ic, k], weight[1, ic, k], ..., weight[31, ic, k]. - These are strided by 256*3 = 768 floats = 3KB. - Not coalesced, but cached in L2. The computation loop: ```cpp float acc = bias[oc]; for (int ic = 0; ic < 256; ic++) { float in_m4 = smem ? smem[ic][p_m4] : x[b][ic][p-4]; float in_0 = smem ? smem[ic][p_0] : x[b][ic][p]; float in_p4 = smem ? smem[ic][p_p4] : x[b][ic][p+4]; float w0 = weight[oc][ic][0]; float w1 = weight[oc][ic][1]; float w2 = weight[oc][ic][2]; acc += w0 * in_m4 + w1 * in_0 + w2 * in_p4; } ``` In the non-shared version, threads in a warp read the same input address for each ic. The warp issues one load instruction, and L1 broadcasts it to all 32 threads. This is extremely efficient! So I think the non-shared memory version might actually be BETTER because: 1. No shared memory overhead (no need to load a tile cooperatively) 2. Very efficient L1 usage due to broadcast loads 3. Higher occupancy (8 blocks per SM) 4. Simpler code Let me verify: For warp 0 (oc=0..31, p=some value): - For ic=0, all threads read x[b, 0, p-4]. Same address. L1 hit after first miss. - Same for ic=1,2,...,255. - Total distinct input addresses per warp per p: 256 * 3 = 768 addresses = 3KB. - L1 cache per SM is 128 KB. With 8 warps per block (256 threads), but each warp accesses different p values. - Warp 1 accesses p+1, so distinct addresses: x[b, ic, p+1-4], x[b, ic, p+1], x[b, ic, p+1+4]. - For 8 warps, the spatial span is p to p+7 (if consecutive positions). - Total distinct spatial positions accessed by 8 warps: (p-4) to (p+7+4) = 16 positions. - Total distinct input addresses per block: 256 channels * 16 positions = 4096 addresses = 16 KB. - This fits perfectly in the 128 KB L1 cache! In fact, with 8 blocks per SM, total input footprint per SM = 8 * 16 KB = 128 KB. This exactly matches the L1 cache size! And since SMs share L2, and the data is reused across blocks, it should work well. This is beautiful. The broadcast nature makes the strided memory access for weights acceptable, and the L1 cache perfectly captures the input reuse. So let's go with the **no-shared-memory** approach for pass 2! Wait, but we still have the issue of applying LayerNorm and GELU before the conv. For each input value x[b, ic, pos], we need to compute: norm = (x - mean[b]) * inv_std[b] * gamma[ic, pos] + beta[ic, pos] gelu_val = gelu(norm) If threads in a warp read the same x[b, ic, pos], they all compute the same norm and gelu. So we can compute this on the fly in the loop: ```cpp for (int ic = 0; ic < 256; ic++) { // All threads in warp read the same x value float x_m4 = x[b][ic][p-4]; // Compute norm + gelu float n_m4 = (x_m4 - mean) * inv_std; float g_m4 = gamma[ic][p-4]; float b_m4 = beta[ic][p-4]; float normed_m4 = n_m4 * g_m4 + b_m4; float gelu_m4 = gelu(normed_m4); // Similarly for p and p+4 ... // Read weights float w0 = weight[oc][ic][0]; ... acc += w0 * gelu_m4 + ...; } ``` But wait, in the inner loop over ic, each thread reads 3 input positions. However, the warp only processes one output position p (for warp 0). All threads in the warp need x at positions p-4, p, p+4. So for a given ic, the warp reads 3 x values. Then it reads gamma and beta at the same 3 positions for that ic. That's 3 reads for x, 3 reads for gamma, 3 reads for beta per ic per warp. But these reads are for the same addresses across the warp (broadcast). And we also read weights: each thread reads 3 weights, 32 threads = 96 weight reads per ic per warp. Memory bandwidth per FMA: - For each output, we do 256*3 = 768 FMAs. - We read 3 x values, 3 gammas, 3 betas, 3 weights (actually 3 weights per thread, but weights are different per thread). - Per output element: 3 x + 3 gamma + 3 beta + 3 weights = 12 floats. - But since the 3 x/gamma/beta are broadcast across the warp, they need to be loaded from DRAM only once per warp, not once per thread. - So per warp output (32 outputs): reads = 3 x + 3 gamma + 3 beta + 32*3 weights = 9 + 96 = 105 floats. - Per output: 105/32 ≈ 3.3 floats. - FLOPs per float: 768*2 / 3.3 ≈ 465 FLOPs per float. This is very compute-intensive in terms of arithmetic intensity! Wait, but the weights are read from DRAM (or L2) per thread. Weights are 786 KB total. L2 cache is 50+ MB. Weights will stay entirely in L2. So weight reads are from L2, not DRAM. DRAM traffic is: - x: read once per block = 16 KB (for the positions accessed by the block) - gamma: read once per block = 16 KB - beta: read once per block = 16 KB - Output: written once per block = 1 KB (32 channels * 8 positions) Total DRAM per block: ~49 KB. Per output element: 49 KB / 256 outputs = 196 bytes / output. That's much more than the "per-thread" arithmetic intensity suggested, because the block accesses 16 KB of input for only 256 outputs. But wait, those 16 KB of input are also accessed by OTHER blocks that handle adjacent positions! Blocks overlap in their input accesses because of the dilation. Block processing positions [0,7] accesses spatial [-4, 11]. Block processing [8,15] accesses [4, 19]. Overlap is 8 positions (4 to 11). So there's significant input reuse across adjacent blocks. However, these blocks might run on different SMs, so the reuse goes through L2 cache, not L1. If L2 captures this reuse: - Total distinct x accessed by all blocks: for all 4096 positions, we need positions [-4, 4099] intersect [0, 4095] = [0, 4095] plus some padding. Actually, all positions 0..4095 are needed, plus some redundant padding. So total x read from DRAM = 128 MB. - Gamma and beta = 8 MB. - Output write = 128 MB. - Total DRAM = 264 MB. Which matches our bandwidth estimate. So the kernel is bandwidth-bound at about 79 microseconds with perfect fusion. Now, PyTorch reference: - LayerNorm: reads x, writes normalized. But it might be fused with GELU internally. Let's assume LayerNorm+GELU reads x once, writes once. Traffic: 128 + 128 = 256 MB. Actually nn.LayerNorm followed by nn.GELU: if not fused, it's 2 passes. PyTorch often fuses these. - Conv1d: reads normalized input, reads weights, writes output. If cuDNN fuses bias, then: input read = 128 MB, output write = 128 MB. Weights are cached. Traffic = 256 MB. - Residual add: reads conv output and x, writes final. Traffic = 128 + 128 + 128 = 384 MB. - But PyTorch might fuse conv + bias + residual if there's a single backward? Not in inference usually. Let me think about whether one kernel is possible instead of two. **One-kernel approach:** If we process the entire batch element in one block or use cooperative groups... Actually, we could use one block per batch element but make it large enough to compute everything. - 32 batch elements, so 32 blocks. - Each block has many threads (e.g., 1024). - Phase 1: 1024 threads reduce 1M elements to mean and var using shared memory. - __syncthreads() - Phase 2: threads compute output. With 1024 threads and 1M outputs per batch element, each thread computes 1024 outputs. 1024 outputs * 768 FMAs = 786k FMAs per thread. That's a lot. With 32 blocks total, only 32 SMs are used (1 block per SM), leaving 100 SMs idle. Bad idea. So multiple blocks per batch element is necessary. And we need the mean/var to be available to all blocks. Two-kernel is the standard approach. Could we use the fact that mean/var are computed and then stored in global memory, with all blocks reading them? Yes, that's the two-kernel approach. What about dynamic parallelism? A kernel launches a kernel. Overhead is too high. Could we have block 0 compute mean/var and then signal other blocks via global memory? We'd need __syncthreads() across blocks, which isn't directly possible. We could use cuda::barrier (cooperative groups), but it's complex and might not be faster. So let's stick with two kernels. Let me estimate the actual runtime for the two-kernel approach: - Pass 1 (reduction): 32 blocks of 1024 threads. Each thread reads 1024 elements. Total reads = 128 MB. With good memory coalescing, this takes about 128 MB / 3.35 TB/s = 38 microseconds. Plus reduction overhead = ~50 microseconds. - Pass 2 (fused): 264 MB / 3.35 TB/s = 79 microseconds. Plus overhead = ~100-150 microseconds. Total: ~150-200 microseconds. Can we do better? Wait, I just realized: `nn.LayerNorm([channels, seq_len])` is LayerNorm over the LAST TWO dimensions. In PyTorch, `LayerNorm(normalized_shape)` normalizes over the last `len(normalized_shape)` dimensions. For input of shape (B, C, L), and normalized_shape=(C, L), it normalizes over dimensions 1 and 2 (C and L). Weight and bias have shape (C, L) = (256, 4096). But `self.norm(x)` where x has shape (B, C, L): - The mean and var are computed over dimensions 1 and 2, i.e., for each batch element, over all C*L values. - The affine transformation multiplies by weight[C, L] and adds bias[C, L]. So yes, weight and bias are full (256, 4096), not per-channel or per-position. Now, is there any computation we can avoid? The LayerNorm is over 1M elements, so the statistics are essentially constant for a given batch element if the input is random. But we still have to compute them. Actually, for a batch of random inputs, the mean is close to 0 and std close to 1... but we can't assume that. We have to compute it exactly. One optimization: if we assume fp32 precision is okay, we can use the `rsqrt` approximation or standard `sqrtf`. Should be fine. Now let me think about the implementation details. **Implementation Plan:** 1. **Pass 1: compute_mean_var kernel** - Grid: (batch_size, 1, 1) = (32, 1, 1) - Block: 1024 threads - Each thread loops over elements from tid to total in steps of 1024 - Accumulates sum and sum_sq in registers - Then shared memory reduction - Writes mean[b] = sum / N and inv_std[b] = 1 / sqrt(sum_sq / N - mean*mean + eps) 2. **Pass 2: fused_forward kernel** - Grid: (B=32, num_channel_tiles=8, num_pos_tiles=512) Wait, 4096/8 = 512, yes. Wait, but the loop structure can be: ```cpp float accum[8] = {bias[oc], ...}; for (int ic = 0; ic < 256; ic++) { // Preload the 16 needed input values for this ic float vals[16]; for (int j = -4; j <= 11; j++) { int p = p_start + j; if (p >= 0 && p < seq_len) { float x_val = x[b][ic][p]; // Apply layer norm and gelu vals[j+4] = gelu((x_val - mean) * inv_std * gamma[ic][p] + beta[ic][p]); } else { vals[j+4] = 0.0f; } } float w0 = weight[oc][ic][0]; float w1 = weight[oc][ic][1]; float w2 = weight[oc][ic][2]; for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) { accum[j] += w0 * vals[j] + w1 * vals[j+4] + w2 * vals[j+8]; } } ``` But preloading 16 vals per ic means 16 reads from global memory per ic. With the broadcast property: - For a fixed ic and warp 0 (py=0, p_start fixed), all threads need the same 16 vals! - All 32 threads in the warp read vals[0] for the same ic. That's 32 threads reading the same address. L1 broadcasts it. - So per ic, the warp reads 16 distinct addresses. - Total reads per warp per ic: 16 x-values + 16 gamma + 16 beta + 96 weights = 144 reads. - But weights are from L2 (cached), and x/gamma/beta are from L1 (after first miss). - Over 256 ic iterations: 256*16 = 4096 distinct x addresses per warp. 16 KB per warp. - With 8 warps per block: 128 KB per block. This exceeds L1 cache! - So L1 cache eviction will occur. Wait, 8 warps, each needing 16 KB of distinct x values = 128 KB. L1 cache per SM is 128 KB. With 8 blocks per SM, that's 1 MB, way more than L1. But blocks don't all run at the same time? Actually up to 8 blocks per SM run concurrently. So concurrent blocks on an SM would need 8 * 128 KB = 1 MB of x cache footprint. This would thrash L1. But let's check: is it 16 KB per warp or per block? Warp py computes positions p_start = block_p_start + py*8. So warp 0: positions block_p_start to block_p_start+7 Warp 1: positions block_p_start+8 to block_p_start+15 ... Warp 7: positions block_p_start+56 to block_p_start+63. For warp 0, needed x positions: block_p_start-4 to block_p_start+11. Span = 16. For warp 1: block_p_start+4 to block_p_start+19. Span = 16. ... For warp 7: block_p_start+52 to block_p_start+67. Span = 16. Total distinct positions for all 8 warps: block_p_start-4 to block_p_start+67. Span = 72 positions. This suggests shared memory IS better after all, for caching the input tile explicitly. But with shared memory, we need the cooperative load. Let me see if the overhead is worth it. For 16,384 blocks: - Each block loads 73.7 KB into shared memory with coalesced access (as I analyzed earlier). - Then the computation phase reads from shared memory (no bank conflicts if laid out properly). - Shared memory bandwidth is ~15 TB/s, much higher than L2/DRAM. So yes, shared memory approach is likely faster. Let me design the shared memory approach carefully. **Shared memory layout:** We need to store the GELU(LayerNorm(x)) values for 256 channels and up to 72 spatial positions. Memory layout: `float smem[256][72]` with padding to avoid bank conflicts. If we use `float smem[256][72]`, then for a fixed channel ic, accessing different positions might have bank conflicts if positions differ by multiples of 32. Actually, a warp accessing smem[ic][p0], smem[ic][p1], ... for different p. If threads in the warp access the same ic and positions p = 0, 8, 16, 24, ... - smem[ic][0] is in bank 0 - smem[ic][8] is in bank 8 - smem[ic][16] is in bank 16 - etc. - Threads 0..7 accessing banks 0,8,16,24,0,8,16,24. Banks 0 and 8 have 2-way conflict! So there are bank conflicts. To avoid bank conflicts, we can add padding: `float smem[256][72+4]` or use a swizzled layout. If we use `smem[ic][p]` with `p` padded to 72, then for p values spaced by 4: - p=0: bank 0 - p=4: bank 4 - p=8: bank 8 - p=12: bank 12 - A warp reading p=0,4,8,12,16,20,24,28 for different output positions: banks 0,4,8,12,16,20,24,28. No conflicts! - What if a warp reads p=0,1,2,3,4,5,6,7? Banks 0..7. No conflicts. - What if a warp reads p=32,33,34,35,36,37,38,39? Banks 0..7. No conflicts. Actually, with 32 banks and float accesses, position p maps to bank `p % 32`. For our conv, each thread in a warp might access 3 positions per output: p-4, p, p+4. If adjacent threads have the same p but different oc (warp layout), they all access the same 3 positions. No bank conflict because it's the same address (broadcast). If adjacent threads have different p (e.g., p=0,1,2,...7), they access banks (p-4)%32, p%32, (p+4)%32. Wait, if all threads in a warp compute the same output position (same p) but different oc, then all threads access the same input positions in shared memory. Broadcast, no conflict. If we organize so that a warp computes the same position for all its threads... but then each warp only computes 32 outputs (1 position * 32 oc). That's what we have with block layout (32 oc, 8 p groups) where each warp handles 1 p group. In block = (32, 8): - Warp 0: oc=0..31, py=0. All threads have same p_start. For a specific output position j within the 8, all threads access the same spatial position in smem (for a given ic and tap). Broadcast, no bank conflict! So with this block layout, there are NO bank conflicts in shared memory for the input tile! Great! Then we should definitely use shared memory. Design for kernel 2 with shared memory: Each block computes a tile of 32 output channels (oc_start to oc_start+31) and 64 output positions (p_start to p_start+63). BlockDim = (32, 8) = 256 threads. - threadIdx.x = 0..31: output channel offset (oc = oc_start + tx) - threadIdx.y = 0..7: position subgroup - Each subgroup handles 8 consecutive positions: p = p_start + py*8 + j, where j is looped over in the thread. Wait, each thread computing 8 positions means we need an inner loop. Alternatively, we can have each thread compute just 1 position by making the block cover 32 channels x 8 positions = 256 outputs. But then we'd need many more blocks. Actually, with block = (32, 8), and each thread computing 8 positions sequentially, the block covers 32 channels x 64 positions = 2048 outputs. This is a good amount of work per block. But we can also have threadIdx.y represent position directly and make the block smaller. Then blocks = (B, C_tiles=8, L_tiles=512) for 8 positions per block. Total 32*8*512 = 131,072 blocks. With each block only doing 256 outputs, that's a lot of blocks but maybe okay. Let me calculate: - 131,072 blocks. - Shared memory per block: for 32 channels x 8 positions tile: - Input needed: 256 channels * (8+8) positions = 4096 floats = 16 KB. - Very little shared memory! - Max blocks per SM = 227 KB / 16 KB = 14 (but thread limited to 8). - Concurrent = 8 * 132 = 1056. - Waves = 131,072 / 1056 = 124 waves. - Each block: 16 KB shared. 8 blocks per SM = 128 KB. Fits! - Each wave: 1056 blocks * 256 outputs = 270,336 outputs. - Total outputs: 33,554,432. - Waves: 124. - Per block compute: 256 outputs * 768 FMAs = 196,608 FMAs. At 67 TFLOPS, compute time = 3ns (theoretical). Memory time for loading 16 KB shared: 16 KB / 3.35 TB/s = 4.8 ns (theoretical). So each block is a few nanoseconds theoretically, but practically maybe 0.5-1 microsecond. 124 waves * 1 us = 124 microseconds. This is fine. But wait, with this small tile, do we have enough并行性 to hide latency? 1056 concurrent blocks seems good. However, each block handles only 8 positions. For a given ic in the loop, the 16 KB shared contains 256 channels x 16 positions. All blocks in the L_tiles dimension share the same input channels but different positions. L2 cache will cache the input, and with blocks running in waves, each wave reads its 16 KB from L2, which might already have it from previous waves if there's locality. Actually, adjacent position tiles need adjacent input, so L2 prefetching or streaming should capture this well. I think both tile sizes (64 positions vs 8 positions) could work. Let me go with **64 positions per block** for fewer blocks and less kernel launch overhead, but with shared memory. Tile: 32 output channels x 64 output positions. Block: (32, 8) threads. Each thread handles 1 output channel and 8 positions. Shared memory: 256 channels x 72 positions = 18,432 floats = 73.7 KB. Weights: loaded from global memory into registers (not shared). Each thread loops over 256 input channels. For each ic, it reads 3 values from shared and 3 weights from global. Wait, earlier I said with block=(32,8) and warp handling same p for all oc, there's no bank conflict. Let me verify: - threadIdx.x = 0..31 (oc offset within tile) - threadIdx.y = 0..7 (py, each handles 8 consecutive positions: py*8 to py*8+7) Warp 0: threads with threadIdx.y=0. So py=0, positions 0..7. oc=0..31. But each thread does 8 positions sequentially! So in the inner loop over j=0..7: - Thread tx computes position p0 = block_p_start + 0*8 + j = block_p_start + j - All threads in warp 0 have py=0, but they iterate through j. - For a fixed j and fixed ic, ALL threads in warp 0 access the same smem[ic][spatial_offset]! - spatial_offset depends on the tap: for tap0, it's (block_p_start + j - 4) - block_p_start + 4 = j (if we index shared from 0) - Wait, let's be precise. If shared stores positions [p_start-4 : p_end+4], that's 72 positions. For output position p = p_start + py*8 + j: - tap0 input position = p - 4 = p_start + py*8 + j - 4 - In shared memory offset = (p_start + py*8 + j - 4) - (p_start - 4) = py*8 + j Similarly: - tap1 offset = py*8 + j + 4 - tap2 offset = py*8 + j + 8 For warp 0 (py=0): - All threads access shared offset j, j+4, j+8 for the same j. - ALL threads access the SAME addresses! Broadcast, no bank conflict. For warp 1 (py=1): - All threads access shared offset 8+j, 12+j, 16+j for the same j. - Again, all threads in the warp access the same addresses. Broadcast, no bank conflict. Perfect! So shared memory layout can be simple: `float smem[256][72]` (or flattened). To avoid bank conflicts for the loading phase, we need coalescing when writing to shared. For loading x into shared: - 256 threads, each loads 72/256 of a channel or similar. - Better: 256 threads collectively load 18,432 values. - 18,432 / 256 = 72 values per thread. - Thread i loads all 72 positions for channel ic = i. - Memory access pattern: thread ic loads x[b, ic, p_start-4+j] for j=0..71. - These are 72 consecutive floats! - Thread 0: base + 0*4096 + p_start-4 (72 consecutive) - Thread 1: base + 1*4096 + p_start-4 (72 consecutive) - Thread 2: base + 2*4096 + p_start-4 (72 consecutive) - A warp of 32 threads loads 72 consecutive floats each, with addresses spaced 4096 floats apart. This access pattern: each thread loads a contiguous chunk, but threads are spaced by 16KB. A warp generates 32 memory transactions of 288 bytes. On H100, this should be serviced efficiently if the memory controller can handle multiple outstanding requests. Actually, 72 floats = 288 bytes. With 128-byte cache lines, each thread touches 3 cache lines. 32 threads * 3 = 96 cache lines. At 128 bytes each = 12,288 bytes requested. Actual data = 32*288 = 9,216 bytes. Some overhead. We can organize the load differently to improve coalescing: - Have each warp load a contiguous chunk of positions for a subset of channels. - E.g., for positions p_start-4 to p_start+27 (32 positions), have warp 0 load channels 0..31 for these 32 positions. - For a fixed position p and channel c, address = base + c*4096 + p. - If we have 32 threads handling 32 channels for the SAME position p: - Thread c reads x[b, c, p]. Address = base + c*4096 + p. - Spaced by 16KB. Not coalesced. - If we have 32 threads handling 32 positions for the SAME channel c: - Thread p reads x[b, c, p_start-4+p]. Address = base + c*4096 + p_start-4+p. - These are 32 consecutive floats! Perfectly coalesced. So: have each warp load 32 consecutive positions for 1 channel. - Warp 0 loads channel 0, positions 0..31 (relative to shared start). - Warp 1 loads channel 0, positions 32..63. - Warp 2 loads channel 0, positions 64..71 (only 8 positions). - Then channel 1, etc. Total warps needed: 256 channels * ceil(72/32) = 256 * 3 = 768 warp-loads. But we only have 8 warps (256 threads) = 256 threads. Each thread belongs to a warp. We can assign warps to different (channel, position_group) pairs. Actually, with 8 warps and 256 channels * 3 position groups = 768 tasks, each warp does 96 tasks. Each task is loading 32 floats (or fewer for the last group). For a task (channel c, pos_group g): - 32 threads in the warp load 32 consecutive positions for channel c. - One thread per position. - Memory: base + c*4096 + (p_start-4) + g*32 + tid. - Coalesced! Then to store in shared memory: - shared offset = c * 72 + g*32 + tid. - Also coalesced if we lay out shared as [channels][72]. So the loading is very efficient. For gamma and beta (same shape as x): - We need to load them during the shared memory load or compute on the fly. - The shared memory stores the GELU(LayerNorm(x)) value, not raw x. - So during the load, thread reads x, gamma, beta, computes norm+gelu, stores result. - Gamma and beta have the same memory layout as x: [channels][seq_len]. - So the exact same coalesced loading pattern applies! This is great. Let me formalize the kernel. **Kernel 2 pseudo-code:** ```cpp #define TILE_OC 32 #define TILE_L 64 #define NUM_IC 256 __global__ void fused_kernel( float* out, const float* x, const float* gamma, const float* beta, const float* weight, const float* bias, const float* mean, const float* inv_std, int B, int C, int L, int kernel_size, int dilation ) { // Block handles 1 batch, TILE_OC out channels, TILE_L positions int b = blockIdx.x; int oc_tile = blockIdx.y; int l_tile = blockIdx.z; int oc_start = oc_tile * TILE_OC; int l_start = l_tile * TILE_L; int tid = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; // 0..255 int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; // Shared memory: [NUM_IC][TILE_L + 8] (need padding space for -4..+4) extern __shared__ float smem[]; const int SMEM_L = TILE_L + 8; // 72 const int smem_size = NUM_IC * SMEM_L; float m = mean[b]; float iv = inv_std[b]; // Phase 1: Load input into shared memory // 256 channels * ceil(72/32)=3 groups = 768 loads per batch. With 8 warps, each does 96. int total_load_tasks = NUM_IC * ((SMEM_L + 31) / 32); // 256 * 3 = 768 for (int task = warp_id; task < total_load_tasks; task += (blockDim.x * blockDim.y / 32)) { int ic = task / ((SMEM_L + 31) / 32); int pg = task % ((SMEM_L + 31) / 32); int pos_in_smem = pg * 32 + lane_id; if (pos_in_smem < SMEM_L) { int actual_pos = l_start - 4 + pos_in_smem; float val = 0.0f; if (actual_pos >= 0 && actual_pos < L) { float xval = x[b * C * L + ic * L + actual_pos]; float g = gamma[ic * L + actual_pos]; float be = beta[ic * L + actual_pos]; float normed = (xval - m) * iv * g + be; val = gelu(normed); } smem[ic * SMEM_L + pos_in_smem] = val; } } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: Compute output int oc = oc_start + threadIdx.x; int py = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 int p_local_start = py * 8; // Each thread handles 8 consecutive positions float bias_val = bias[oc]; float accum[8]; for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) accum[j] = bias_val; for (int ic = 0; ic < NUM_IC; ic++) { float w0 = weight[(oc * NUM_IC + ic) * 3 + 0]; float w1 = weight[(oc * NUM_IC + ic) * 3 + 1]; float w2 = weight[(oc * NUM_IC + ic) * 3 + 2]; for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) { int p_smem = p_local_start + j; // smem position for tap1 float in0 = smem[ic * SMEM_L + p_smem]; // offset 0 in conv sense, but corresponds to tap1? Need to think. // Actually: // For output position l_start + p_local_start + j: // tap0 (offset -4): input at l_start + p_local_start + j - 4 // in smem: (l_start + p_local_start + j - 4) - (l_start - 4) = p_local_start + j // tap1 (offset 0): input at l_start + p_local_start + j // in smem: p_local_start + j + 4 // tap2 (offset +4): input at l_start + p_local_start + j + 4 // in smem: p_local_start + j + 8 float in_m4 = smem[ic * SMEM_L + (p_local_start + j)]; // tap -4 float in_0 = smem[ic * SMEM_L + (p_local_start + j + 4)]; // tap 0 float in_p4 = smem[ic * SMEM_L + (p_local_start + j + 8)]; // tap +4 accum[j] += w0 * in_m4 + w1 * in_0 + w2 * in_p4; } } // Add residual and write output for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) { int actual_pos = l_start + p_local_start + j; if (actual_pos < L) { float res = x[b * C * L + oc * L + actual_pos]; out[b * C * L + oc * L + actual_pos] = accum[j] + res; } } } ``` Wait, I need to verify the padding/offset logic. The conv formula for output position `pos_out`: `out[b, oc, pos_out] = sum_ic sum_k weight[oc, ic, k] * input_padded[b, ic, pos_out + k * dilation - padding]` With padding = dilation = 4, k = 0,1,2: `input_pos = pos_out + k * 4 - 4` k=0: pos_out - 4 k=1: pos_out k=2: pos_out + 4 The shared memory stores `smem[ic][s]` where `s = actual_input_pos - (l_start - 4)`. So `actual_input_pos = l_start - 4 + s`. For output position `pos_out = l_start + p_local_start + j`: - k=0: actual_input_pos = pos_out - 4 = l_start + p_local_start + j - 4 s = (l_start + p_local_start + j - 4) - (l_start - 4) = p_local_start + j - k=1: actual_input_pos = pos_out = l_start + p_local_start + j s = p_local_start + j + 4 - k=2: actual_input_pos = pos_out + 4 = l_start + p_local_start + j + 4 s = p_local_start + j + 8 Yes! So: - tap0 (k=0) -> smem[p_local_start + j] - tap1 (k=1) -> smem[p_local_start + j + 4] - tap2 (k=2) -> smem[p_local_start + j + 8] This is correct. And since `p_local_start = py * 8`, for py=0, positions j=0..7 map to s=0..7, 4..11, 8..15. The maximum s accessed is 15, which is within SMEM_L=72. For py=7, positions j=0..7 map to s=56..63, 60..67, 64..71. Maximum s=71, which is within 72. Good. Now, the residual addition: `x[b, oc, actual_pos]`. This is the original input. Memory access pattern for writing output: Each thread writes 8 positions for its oc. Thread (oc, py): writes positions l_start + py*8 + j for j=0..7. Oc is threadIdx.x. Threads 0..31 have different oc but same positions. Address: b*C*L + oc*L + actual_pos. For fixed actual_pos, threads 0..31 write to addresses spaced by L=4096. This is strided but should be fine. Standard fast GELU: `gelu(x) = 0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3)))` Yes, this is what many implementations use, including possibly PyTorch. `tanh` is faster than `erff`. Let me use: ```cpp inline __device__ float gelu(float x) { const float c = 0.044715f; const float sqrt_2_over_pi = 0.7978845608f; float x3 = x * x * x; float t = tanhf(sqrt_2_over_pi * (x + c * x3)); return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + t); } ``` Wait, PyTorch might use `1.702 * x` or something for the sigmoid approximation? No, that's for GELU with the sigmoid approximation? Let me think... PyTorch `nn.GELU` exact: `x * Φ(x)` where `Φ` is the CDF of standard normal. Approximation: `x * 0.5 * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/π) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3)))` I think PyTorch uses the `tanh` approximation by default in CUDA for speed. Let me use the exact same approximation. Actually, for numerical equivalence, we should match whatever PyTorch does. Since PyTorch might use the tanh approximation on CUDA, we'll be matching it. Yes. I think recent PyTorch uses the `tanh` approximation for fp32 on GPU. Let me use it. Now for the LayerNorm. The exact formula is: `y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias` Where `var` is the unbiased or population variance? PyTorch uses population variance (divides by N, not N-1). So `inv_std = 1 / sqrt(var + eps)` where `var = sum((x - mean)^2) / N`. Yes. Now let me also think about whether I can avoid the two-kernel launch overhead by computing the reduction inline but using a smaller shared memory tile. Actually, another idea: What if I compute the mean and variance on the fly using grid-stride loops in a separate reduction kernel, but I can also compute partial sums in the first kernel and then have the second kernel read them? With 32 blocks and 1024 threads each: ```cpp __global__ void compute_stats( const float* x, float* mean, float* inv_std, int B, int C, int L ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int N = C * L; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; const float* ptr = x + b * N; for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float val = ptr[i]; sum += val; sum_sq += val * val; } // Warp reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float s_sum[32]; __shared__ float s_sq[32]; if (tid % 32 == 0) { s_sum[tid / 32] = sum; s_sq[tid / 32] = sum_sq; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32) { sum = (tid < blockDim.x / 32) ? s_sum[tid] : 0.0f; sum_sq = (tid < blockDim.x / 32) ? s_sq[tid] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } if (tid == 0) { float m = sum / N; float var = sum_sq / N - m * m; mean[b] = m; inv_std[b] = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); } } } ``` Wait, blockDim.x is 1024. So blockDim.x / 32 = 32 warps. The second reduction needs warp 0 to reduce 32 values. But warp 0 only has 32 threads, and I can just do another shuffle reduction with the 32 threads. Actually, I can streamline this: - Each of the 32 warps does a reduction to lane 0. - Then warp 0 reads the 32 partial sums and reduces them. - This is standard and efficient. For 1M elements and 1024 threads, each thread reads 1024 elements. The loop is: ```cpp for (int i = tid; i < N; i += 1024) { float v = ptr[i]; sum += v; sum_sq += v * v; } ``` This is very coalesced (each thread reads consecutive elements with stride 1024). 1024 threads * 1024 elements = 1,048,576. Wait, yes, 1024*1024 = 1,048,576. Address for thread i: b*N + i, then b*N + i + 1024, etc. Thread 0: 0, 1024, 2048, ... Thread 1: 1, 1025, 2049, ... That's perfectly coalesced! Next iteration: thread 0 reads 1024, thread 1 reads 1025, ..., also coalesced. So the reduction kernel has coalesced reads. Good. One concern: the `load_inline` overhead. Since the CUDA code is compiled at module load time, it might take a few seconds, but that's fine. Now let me write the actual Python module with `load_inline`. ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #define TILE_OC 32 #define TILE_L 64 #define NUM_IC 256 #define SMEM_L (TILE_L + 8) #define EPS 1e-5f inline __device__ float gelu_tanh(float x) { const float c = 0.044715f; const float sqrt_2_over_pi = 0.7978845608f; float x3 = x * x * x; float t = tanhf(sqrt_2_over_pi * (x + c * x3)); return 0.5f * x * (1.0f + t); } __global__ void compute_stats_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ mean, float* __restrict__ inv_std, int B, int N ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; float sum = 0.0f; float sum_sq = 0.0f; const float* ptr = x + b * N; #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = tid; i < N; i += blockDim.x) { float val = ptr[i]; sum += val; sum_sq += val * val; } // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); sum_sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_sq, offset); } __shared__ float s_sum[32]; turn 2llm latency: 805.5s
reasoning
The issue is that the CUDA functions need to be declared in a header file (cpp_sources) that is included before the pybind wrapper is generated. When using load_inline with `cpp_sources=""`, the auto-generated pybind module can't find the declarations. I need to put the function declarations in `cpp_sources`. Looking at the error: the functions are declared in the .cu file but the pybind generation happens in the .cpp file which doesn't include the declarations. Let me restructure to have the declarations in `cpp_sources` and the implementations in `cuda_sources`.

Actually, you know what could work really well? Since kernel_size=3, I can write the conv+add kernel very simply: ```cuda __global__ void conv_add_kernel( float* out, const float* x, const float* w, const float* b, const float* residual, int B, int C, int L) { int b = blockIdx.x; int oc = blockIdx.y * TILE_OC + threadIdx.x; if (oc >= C) return; int l = blockIdx.z * TILE_L + threadIdx.y; if (l >= L) return; float sum = b[oc]; for (int ic = 0; ic < C; ic++) { int idx_ic = b*C*L + ic*L; float in0 = (l >= 4) ? x[idx_ic + l - 4] : 0; float in1 = x[idx_ic + l]; float in2 = (l + 4 < L) ? x[idx_ic + l + 4] : 0; int w_idx = ((oc * C) + ic) * 3; sum += w[w_idx] * in0 + w[w_idx+1] * in1 + w[w_idx+2] * in2; } int out_idx = b*C*L + oc*L + l; out[out_idx] = sum + residual[out_idx]; } ``` This is very simple but each thread does C=256 iterations, each reading 3 input values and 3 weights. For block size (32, 8), each block computes 32*8 = 256 output points. The total memory reads per block: - 256 output points * 256 channels * (3 inputs + 3 weights) ≈ + bias reads - Each input is read by multiple threads Actually, each input at position l is read by threads computing output at l (as in1), l+4 (as in0), and l-4 (as in2). So each input is read 3 times. For C=256, L=4096, B=32: - Total input reads = 3 * B * C * L = 3 * 32 * 256 * 4096 = 100,663,296 floats - Total weight reads = B * C * C * 3 = 32 * 256 * 256 * 3 = 6,291,456 floats - Total bias reads = B * C * L = 32 * 256 * 4096 = 33,554,432 floats - Total residual reads = B * C * L = 33,554,432 floats - Total writes = B * C * L = 33,554,432 floats Total memory traffic = 207,093,760 floats = 828 MB. At 3.35 TB/s, that's ~247 microseconds. But with 25.8 GFLOPs, at 67 TFLOPS that's ~385 microseconds. So it's compute bound! Hmm, but actually we can do shared memory tiling. If each block computes 32 output channels and 64 positions, we can cache the inputs for those positions across all input channels. But with C=256, caching all input channels might be too much. Wait, an even better approach: process a group of output channels together, but stream through input channels in tiles. Let's say a block computes 32 output channels. Load weight for those 32 output channels and all 256 input channels: 32 * 256 * 3 = 24,576 floats = 98 KB. That fits in shared memory! Then for each output position l, the thread only needs to read 3 input floats per input channel, but if we're processing all 256 input channels, we can load the needed inputs into shared memory in tiles. However, for each l, we need 256 * 3 = 768 input floats. With shared memory of ~200KB, we can load ~50,000 floats. So we can load inputs for a range of positions. Actually, a simpler shared memory optimization: for a block working on output positions [l_start, l_start + TILE_L), cache the inputs for all C channels across [l_start - 4, l_start + TILE_L + 4). For TILE_L = 64: we need C * (64 + 8) = 256 * 72 = 18,432 floats = 73 KB. That's exactly what I had before! And with this approach, memory traffic is reduced because each input is loaded once from global memory and reused across all output channels. The weights are also loaded once per block. And actually, for the weights: if a block computes 32 output channels, each output channel needs weights for all 256 input channels * 3 kernel positions = 768 floats. For 32 output channels: 32 * 768 = 24,576 floats = 98 KB. We can keep weights in shared memory or registers. Actually, with 256 threads and 32 output channels, each thread could hold weights for one output channel in registers (or local memory). 768 floats * 4 bytes = 3 KB per thread. That's way too much for registers! So better to keep weights in shared memory or stream them from global memory. If we stream weights, coalesced reads from global memory are fine. With 256 threads (32*8), if each thread handles one output channel and 8 positions: - 32 threads handle 32 output channels - 8 positions per thread = 64 positions total - Each thread does C=256 iterations - In each iteration, all 32 threads read the same weight (for different output channels but same input channel and kernel position). Wait, no - they read different weights for their respective output channels. Actually, for weights: if threads 0-31 each handle a different output channel, and they all read weights for input channel ic at the same time, the weight reads for oc=0..31 are contiguous in memory. Specifically: weight[oc*C*3 + ic*3 + k] for oc=0..31 are at addresses [0, C*3, 2*C*3, ..., 31*C*3] + ic*3 + k With C=256, C*3=768. So these are 768 floats apart. That's a stride of 768*4 = 3072 bytes. With 32 threads, each warp would read 32 elements with stride 3072 bytes. That's a memory transaction of 32*4=128 bytes per thread, but with huge strides. Actually, this is terrible for coalescing. If we transpose the weight storage to [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size] instead, then for a fixed ic and k, weight[ic, oc, k] for oc=0..31 are contiguous. But PyTorch uses [out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size]. We could transpose on the Python side! Yes! If we transpose the weight to [C, C, 3] -> [C, C*3] and then further to [C, 3, C]... actually, let's think. For better memory access, we want threads in a warp to read contiguous memory. If thread `tid` handles output channel `tid`, and we read weight for input channel `ic`: Current layout: W[oc][ic][k] stored at W + (oc*C + ic)*3 + k - Thread 0 reads W + (0*C + ic)*3 + k = W + ic*3 + k - Thread 1 reads W + (1*C + ic)*3 + k = W + C*3 + ic*3 + k These are C*3 = 768 floats apart. Not coalesced. Alternative layout: W[ic][k][oc] stored at W + (ic*3 + k)*C + oc - Thread 0 reads W + (ic*3 + k)*C + 0 - Thread 1 reads W + (ic*3 + k)*C + 1 These ARE contiguous! Perfect coalescing. So if we transpose the weight on the Python side before passing to CUDA, we can get much better memory bandwidth. But wait, is this the performance bottleneck? / N - m * m;\n mean[b] = m;\n invstd[b] = 1.0f / sqrtf(v + 1e-5f);\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void norm_gelu_kernel(float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ x, \n const float* __restrict__ gamma, torch::Tensor mean, torch::Tensor invstd, int B, int N) {\n dim3 grid(B, 1, 1);\n dim3 block(1024, 1, 1);\n stats_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(x.data_ptr<float>(), mean.data_ptr<float>(), invstd.data_ptr<float>(), B, N);\n}\n\nvoid launch_norm_gelu(torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor gamma, torch::Tensor beta, \n torch::Tensor mean, torch::Tensor invstd, int B, int N) {\n Actually, `__shfl_down_sync` only works within the same warp, and all active threads in the warp must call it. If only thread 0 enters the `if`, the other threads in warp 0 don't reach the `__shfl_down_sync`, which could cause issues. The correct CUDA pattern for a final reduction across all values held by the first warp is: ```cuda if (tid < num_warps) { s = sm[tid]; sq = sqv[tid]; } else { s = 0.0f; sq = 0.0f; } // All lanes in warp 0 participate for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { s += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, s, off); sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, off); } if (tid == 0) { mean[b] = s / N; invstd[b] = 1.0f / sqrtf(sq / N - mean[b] * mean[b] + 1e-5f); } ``` So the first bug is: `if (tid == 0)` wrapping the shfl_down is wrong. It should be `if (tid < 32)` or no if statement at all (since only warp 0 threads should be here, but actually all threads from 0 to 1023 will reach the code unless guarded). Wait, if I use `if (tid < 32)`, then all 32 threads in warp 0 call `__shfl_down_sync`, which is correct. This is the bug! Let me fix it. Actually, even simpler: after `__syncthreads()`, I can have: ```cuda if (tid < 32) { s = (tid < num_warps) ? sm[tid] : 0.0f; sq = (tid < num_warps) ? sqv[tid] : 0.0f; for (int off = 16; off > 0; off >>= 1) { s += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, s, off); sq += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sq, off); } if (tid == 0) { float m = s / N; float v = sq / N - m * m; mean[b] = m; invstd[b] = 1.0f / sqrtf(v + 1e-5f); } } ``` This is correct. All 32 threads in warp 0 participate, with padded zeros for unused lanes. Let me also fix the `norm_gelu` to use `erff` for exact GELU. Actually, wait: do I even need a custom kernel for conv1d?
